                }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Multiple wheat genomes reveal global variation in modern breeding"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2020 588:7837"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-11-25"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Advances in genomics have expedited&#160;the improvement of several agriculturally important crops but similar efforts in wheat (Triticum spp.) have been more challenging. This is largely owing to the size and complexity of the wheat genome1, and the lack of genome-assembly data for multiple wheat lines2,3. Here we generated ten chromosome pseudomolecule and five scaffold assemblies of hexaploid wheat to explore the genomic diversity among wheat lines from global breeding programs. Comparative analysis revealed extensive structural rearrangements, introgressions from wild relatives and differences in gene content resulting from complex breeding histories aimed at improving adaptation to diverse environments, grain yield and quality, and resistance to stresses4,5. We provide examples outlining the utility of these genomes, including a detailed multi-genome-derived nucleotide-binding leucine-rich repeat protein repertoire involved in disease resistance and the characterization of Sm16, a gene associated with insect resistance. These genome assemblies will provide a basis for functional gene discovery and breeding to deliver the next generation of modern wheat cultivars. Comparison of multiple genome assemblies from wheat reveals extensive diversity that results from the complex breeding history of wheat and provides a basis for further potential improvements to this important food crop."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Advances in genomics have expedited the improvement of several agriculturally important crops but similar efforts in wheat (<i>Triticum</i> spp.) have been more challenging. This is largely owing to the size and complexity of the wheat genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2193">1</a></sup>, and the lack of genome-assembly data for multiple wheat lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Montenegro, J. D. et al. The pangenome of hexaploid bread wheat. Plant J. 90, 1007–1013 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2197">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium (IWGSC). A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2200">3</a></sup>. Here we generated ten chromosome pseudomolecule and five scaffold assemblies of hexaploid wheat to explore the genomic diversity among wheat lines from global breeding programs. Comparative analysis revealed extensive structural rearrangements, introgressions from wild relatives and differences in gene content resulting from complex breeding histories aimed at improving adaptation to diverse environments, grain yield and quality, and resistance to stresses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="He, F. et al. Exome sequencing highlights the role of wild-relative introgression in shaping the adaptive landscape of the wheat genome. Nat. Genet. 51, 896–904 (2019); correction 51, 1194 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2204">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Pont, C. et al. Tracing the ancestry of modern bread wheats. Nat. Genet. 51, 905–911 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2207">5</a></sup>. We provide examples outlining the utility of these genomes, including a detailed multi-genome-derived nucleotide-binding leucine-rich repeat protein repertoire involved in disease resistance and the characterization of <i>Sm1</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Kassa, M. T. et al. A saturated SNP linkage map for the orange wheat blossom midge resistance gene Sm1. Theor. Appl. Genet. 129, 1507–1517 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2213">6</a></sup>, a gene associated with insect resistance. These genome assemblies will provide a basis for functional gene discovery and breeding to deliver the next generation of modern wheat cultivars.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Wheat is a staple food across all parts of the world and is one of the most widely grown and consumed crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Tadesse, W. et al. Genetic gains in wheat breeding and its role in feeding the world. Crop Breed. Genet. Genom. 1, e190005 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2239">7</a></sup>. As the human population continues to grow, wheat production must increase by more than 50% over current levels by 2050 to meet demand<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Tadesse, W. et al. Genetic gains in wheat breeding and its role in feeding the world. Crop Breed. Genet. Genom. 1, e190005 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2243">7</a></sup>. Efforts to increase wheat production may be aided by comprehensive genomic resources from global breeding programs to identify within-species allelic diversity and determine the best allele combinations to produce superior cultivars<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Montenegro, J. D. et al. The pangenome of hexaploid bread wheat. Plant J. 90, 1007–1013 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2247">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Zhao, Q. et al. Pan-genome analysis highlights the extent of genomic variation in cultivated and wild rice. Nat. Genet. 50, 278–284 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2250">8</a></sup>.</p><p>Two species dominate current global wheat production: allotetraploid (AABB) durum wheat (<i>Triticum turgidum</i> ssp. <i>durum</i>), which is used to make couscous and pasta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Dubcovsky, J. &amp; Dvorak, J. Genome plasticity a key factor in the success of polyploid wheat under domestication. Science 316, 1862–1866 (2007)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2263">9</a></sup>, and allohexaploid (AABBDD) bread wheat (<i>Triticum aestivum</i>), used for making bread and noodles. A, B and D in these designations correspond to separate subgenomes derived from three ancestral diploid species with similar but distinct genome structure and gene content that diverged between 2.5 and 6 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Marcussen, T. et al. Ancient hybridizations among the ancestral genomes of bread wheat. Science 345, 1250092 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2270">10</a></sup>. The large genome size (16 Gb for bread wheat), high sequence similarity between subgenomes and abundance of repetitive elements (about 85% of the genome) hampered early wheat genome-assembly efforts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium (IWGSC). A chromosome-based draft sequence of the hexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2275">3</a></sup>. However, chromosome-level assemblies have recently become available for both tetraploid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93–97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2279">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Maccaferri, M. et al. Durum wheat genome highlights past domestication signatures and future improvement targets. Nat. Genet. 51, 885–895 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2282">12</a></sup> and hexaploid wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2286">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. The first near-complete assembly of the hexaploid bread wheat genome, Triticum aestivum. Gigascience 6, 1–7 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2289">13</a></sup>. Although these genome assemblies are valuable resources, they do not fully capture within-species genomic variation that can be used for crop improvement, and comparative genome data from multiple individuals is still needed to expedite bread wheat research and breeding. Until now, comparative genomics of multiple bread wheat lines have been limited to exome-capture sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="He, F. et al. Exome sequencing highlights the role of wild-relative introgression in shaping the adaptive landscape of the wheat genome. Nat. Genet. 51, 896–904 (2019); correction 51, 1194 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2293">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Pont, C. et al. Tracing the ancestry of modern bread wheats. Nat. Genet. 51, 905–911 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2296">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Winfield, M. O. et al. Targeted re-sequencing of the allohexaploid wheat exome. Plant Biotechnol. J. 10, 733–742 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2299">14</a></sup>, low-coverage sequencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Montenegro, J. D. et al. The pangenome of hexaploid bread wheat. Plant J. 90, 1007–1013 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2303">2</a></sup> and whole-genome scaffolded assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Zimin, A. V. et al. The first near-complete assembly of the hexaploid bread wheat genome, Triticum aestivum. Gigascience 6, 1–7 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2307">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Arora, D., Gross, T. &amp; Brueggeman, R. Allele characterization of genes required for rpg4-mediated wheat stem rust resistance identifies Rpg5 as the R gene. Phytopathology 103, 1153–1161 (2013)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2310">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Adamski, N. M. et al. A roadmap for gene functional characterisation in crops with large genomes: lessons from polyploid wheat. eLife 9, e55646 (2020)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2310_1">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Uauy, C. Wheat genomics comes of age. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 36, 142–148 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2313">17</a></sup>. Here we report multiple reference-quality genome assemblies and explore genome variation that, owing to past breeder selection, differs greatly between bread wheat lines. These genome assemblies usher a new era for bread wheat and equip researchers and breeders with the tools needed to improve bread wheat and meet future food demands.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Global variation in wheat genomes"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Global variation in wheat genomes</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>To expand on the genome assembly of wheat for Chinese Spring<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2325">1</a></sup>, we generated ten reference-quality pseudomolecule assemblies (RQAs) and five scaffold-level assemblies of hexaploid wheat (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">1</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">3</a>). For each RQA, we performed de novo assembly of contigs (contig N50 &gt; 48 kb) that were combined into scaffolds (N50 &gt; 10 Mb) spanning more than 14.2 Gb (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">1</a>). The completeness of the genomes was supported by a universal single-copy orthologue (BUSCO) analysis that identified more than 97% of the expected gene content in each genome (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">1</a>). More than 94% of the scaffolds were ordered, oriented and curated using 10X Genomics linked reads and three-dimensional chromosome conformation capture sequencing (Hi-C) to generate 21 pseudomolecules, as done previously for wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2345">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Maccaferri, M. et al. Durum wheat genome highlights past domestication signatures and future improvement targets. Nat. Genet. 51, 885–895 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2348">12</a></sup> and barley (<i>Hordeum vulgare</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427–433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2355">18</a></sup>. The size and structure of the genomes were similar to that of Chinese Spring, and we observed high collinearity between the pseudomolecules (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig4">1</a>). We also independently validated the scaffold placement and orientation in the pseudomolecule assembly of CDC Landmark by Oxford Nanopore long-read sequencing (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig5">2a</a>, Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">2</a>). To complement the RQAs, we generated scaffold-level assemblies of five additional bread wheat lines (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">1</a>). To determine the global context of the 15 assemblies, we combined our data with existing datasets<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="He, F. et al. Exome sequencing highlights the role of wild-relative introgression in shaping the adaptive landscape of the wheat genome. Nat. Genet. 51, 896–904 (2019); correction 51, 1194 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2372">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Pont, C. et al. Tracing the ancestry of modern bread wheats. Nat. Genet. 51, 905–911 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2375">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Edwards, D. et al. Bread matters: a national initiative to profile the genetic diversity of Australian wheat. Plant Biotechnol. J. 10, 703–708 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2378">19</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig1">1a</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">4</a>). The genetic relationships were in agreement with those reported in previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="He, F. et al. Exome sequencing highlights the role of wild-relative introgression in shaping the adaptive landscape of the wheat genome. Nat. Genet. 51, 896–904 (2019); correction 51, 1194 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2388">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Pont, C. et al. Tracing the ancestry of modern bread wheats. Nat. Genet. 51, 905–911 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2391">5</a></sup> and reflected pedigree, geographical location and growth habit (that is, spring versus winter type). There was also a clear separation between the newly assembled genomes and Chinese Spring, supporting that they capture geographical and historical variation not represented in the Chinese Spring assembly.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Patterns of variation in the wheat genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Patterns of variation in the wheat genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="501" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Principal component analysis of polymorphisms from exome-capture sequencing of about 1,200 lines (grey markers), 16 lines from whole-genome shotgun resequencing (orange markers) and our new assemblies (black markers). Text colours reflect different geographical locations and winter or spring growth. <b>b</b>, Dendrogram of pairwise Jaccard similarities for gene PAV between all RQA assemblies. <b>c</b>, Number of unique NLRs at different per cent identity cut-offs as the number of genomes increases. Dashed vertical lines represent 90% of the NLR complement. Markers indicate the mean values of all permutations of the order of adding genomes. Whiskers show maximum and minimum values based on one million random permutations. <b>d</b>, Chromosomal location versus insertion age distribution of unique to (reading downward) increasingly shared syntenic full-length LTR retrotransposons.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec3" data-title="Polyploidy and CNV drive gene diversification"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec3-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec3">Polyploidy and CNV drive gene diversification</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec3-content"><p>Single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs), insertions or deletions (indels), presence/absence variation (PAV) and gene copy number variation (CNV) influence agronomically important traits. This is particularly true for polyploid species such as wheat, in which gene redundancy can buffer the effect of genome variation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Uauy, C. Wheat genomics comes of age. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 36, 142–148 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2435">17</a></sup>. To assess gene content, we projected around 107,000 high-confidence gene models from Chinese Spring<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2439">1</a></sup> onto the RQAs (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">3</a>). The total number of projected genes exhibited a narrow range, between 118,734 and 120,967 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">5</a>). We identified orthologous groups among projected genes and used the alignment of the orthologous groups to examine SNPs in coding sequences (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">3</a>). The peak positions of nucleotide diversity across the three subgenomes were highly similar to those reported in previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Jordan, K. W. et al. A haplotype map of allohexaploid wheat reveals distinct patterns of selection on homoeologous genomes. Genome Biol. 16, 48 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2453">20</a></sup>, supporting a strong representation of breeding diversity within the RQAs (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig6">3a, b</a>). The correlation of synonymous nucleotide diversity <i>π</i> (<i>r</i> = 0.11–0.29) and Tajima’s <i>D</i> (<i>r</i> = 0.02–0.06) between homeologues was low (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">6</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">8</a>). This suggested that polyploidization increased the number of targets of selection and contributed to broad adaptation of bread wheat, as in wild polyploid plant species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jordan, K. W. et al. A haplotype map of allohexaploid wheat reveals distinct patterns of selection on homoeologous genomes. Genome Biol. 16, 48 (2015)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2479">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Paape, T. et al. Patterns of polymorphism and selection in the subgenomes of the allopolyploid Arabidopsis kamchatica. Nat. Commun. 9, 3909 (2018)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2479_1">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Paape, T. et al. Conserved but attenuated parental gene expression in allopolyploids: Constitutive zinc hyperaccumulation in the allotetraploid Arabidopsis kamchatica. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 2781–2800 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2482">22</a></sup>. Further investigation of orthologous groups indicated that 88.1% were unambiguous (clusters containing at most one member in each cultivar) (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig6">3c</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">5</a>). Orthologous groups comprising exactly one gene in each line (‘complete’) were the most frequent (approximately 73.5% of genes per cultivar), suggesting strong retention of orthologous genes within the ten RQAs. The residual genes represented either singleton genes with no reciprocal best BLAST hits or genes located in complex clusters in at least one cultivar. Roughly 12% of genes showed PAVs, and their clustering resulted in relationships (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig1">1b</a>) that were consistent with SNP-based phylogenetic similarities (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig1">1a</a>). In addition, approximately 26% of the projected genes were found in tandem duplications, indicating that CNV is a strong contributor of genetic variation in wheat.</p><p>To provide an example of gene expansion on emerging breeding targets, we performed a more detailed analysis of the restorer of fertility (<i>Rf</i>) gene families (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">4</a>). <i>Rf</i> genes are involved in restoring pollen fertility in hybrid breeding programs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Melonek, J., Stone, J. D. &amp; Small, I. Evolutionary plasticity of restorer-of-fertility-like proteins in rice. Sci. Rep. 6, 35152 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2511">23</a></sup>, and we identified a previously undescribed clade within the mitochondrial transcription termination factor (mTERF) family (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">9</a>), which has recently been implicated in fertility restoration in barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Bernhard, T., Koch, M., Snowdon, R. J., Friedt, W. &amp; Wittkop, B. Undesired fertility restoration in msm1 barley associates with two mTERF genes. Theor. Appl. Genet. 132, 1335–1350 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2519">24</a></sup>. Of note, this clade shows evolutionary patterns similar to those of <i>Rf-</i>like pentatricopeptide repeat (PPR) proteins, representatives of which are associated with <i>Rf3</i>, a major locus used in hybrid wheat breeding programs (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig7">4</a>). Although wheat is currently not a hybrid crop, there is substantial interest in <i>Rf</i> genes and their potential application in hybrid wheat production systems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Whitford, R. et al. Hybrid breeding in wheat: technologies to improve hybrid wheat seed production. J. Exp. Bot. 64, 5411–5428 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2535">25</a></sup>. To our knowledge, no <i>Rf</i> genes have been cloned in wheat and our analysis of <i>Rf</i> genes in multiple RQAs and identification of an <i>Rf</i> clade in wheat is an important step forward in tackling the challenges of hybrid wheat breeding.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec4" data-title="The wheat NLR repertoire"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec4-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec4">The wheat NLR repertoire</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec4-content"><p>To further exemplify the use of multi-genome comparisons for characterizing agronomically relevant gene families, we examined gene expansion in nucleotide-binding leucine-rich repeat (NLR) proteins, which are major components of the innate immune system and are often causal genes for disease resistance in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Keller, B., Wicker, T. &amp; Krattinger, S. G. Advances in wheat and pathogen genomics: Implications for disease control. Annu. Rev. Phytopathol. 56, 67–87 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2557">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Steuernagel, B. et al. Rapid cloning of disease-resistance genes in plants using mutagenesis and sequence capture. Nat. Biotechnol. 34, 652–655 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2560">27</a></sup>. We performed de novo annotation of loci that contain conserved NLR motifs (NB-ARC–leucine-rich repeat) and identified around 2,500 loci with NLR signatures in each RQA (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">10</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">11</a>). A redundancy analysis showed that only 31–34% of the NLR signatures are shared across all genomes, and the number of unique signatures ranged from 22 to 192 per wheat cultivar. We estimated the number of unique NLR signatures that can be detected by incrementally adding more wheat genomes to the dataset; this revealed that 90% of the NLR complement is reached at between 8 (considering 95% sequence identity) and 11 wheat lines (considering 100% protein sequence identity) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig1">1c</a>). The total NLR complement of all wheat lines consisted of 5,905 (98% identity) to 7,780 (100% identity) unique NLR signatures, highlighting the size and complexity of the repertoire of receptors involved in disease resistance.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec5" data-title="Transposon signatures identify introgressions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec5-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec5">Transposon signatures identify introgressions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec5-content"><p>Transposable elements make up a large majority of the wheat genome and have a critical role in genome structure and gene regulation. We characterized the overall transposable element content (81.6%) and its composition (69% long terminal-repeat retrotransposons (LTR) and 12.5% DNA transposons) in the RQAs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">5</a>). Across all RQAs, we annotated 1.22 × 10<sup>6</sup> full length (fl)-LTRs, which clustered lines into the same groups we observed from our analysis of PAV and SNPs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig1">1a, b</a>, Extended Data <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig6">Fig. 3d</a>). Generally, unique fl-LTRs (147,450) were young (median of 0.9 million years) and were enriched in the highly recombining, more distal chromosomal regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig1">1d</a>). By contrast, shared fl-LTRs were older (median of 1.3 million years) and were more evenly distributed across the pericentric regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig1">1d</a>). The RLC-<i>Angela</i> fl-LTRs were the most abundant (21,000–27,000 full-length copies per genome) and analysis of variant patterns identified several chromosomal segments that contained numerous unique or rare retrotransposon insertions (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig8">5</a>), which, on the basis of breeding history, we hypothesize to represent introgressions. For example, the LongReach Lancer RQA revealed two unique regions, a pericentric region on chromosome 2B and a segment on the end of chromosome 3D (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig2">2a, b</a>), both of which affect chromosome length (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig8">5</a>). We used pedigree analysis to postulate the source of the introgressions and performed whole-genome sequencing of multiple accessions of putative donors. LongReach Lancer carries the stem rust resistance gene <i>Sr36</i>, derived from an introgression from <i>Triticum timopheevii</i>, and the resistance genes <i>Lr24</i> (leaf rust) and <i>Sr24</i> (stem rust), derived from tall wheatgrass<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Bariana, H. S. et al. Mapping of durable adult plant and seedling resistances to stripe rust and stem rust diseases in wheat. Aust. J. Agric. Res. 52, 1247–1255 (2001)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2624">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Chemayek, B. et al. Tight repulsion linkage between Sr36 and Sr39 was revealed by genetic, cytogenetic and molecular analyses. Theor. Appl. Genet. 130, 587–595 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2627">29</a></sup> (<i>Thinopyrum ponticum</i>). We generated whole-genome sequence reads from multiple <i>T. ponticum</i> and <i>T. timopheevii</i> accessions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">12</a>) and alignment to the LongReach Lancer RQA confirmed a <i>T. ponticum</i> introgression spanning a region of approximately 60 Mb of chromosome 3D (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig2">2a</a>), whereas <i>T. timopheevii</i> aligned to the majority (427 Mb) of chromosome 2B (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig2">2b</a>). Overall, we identified 341 chromosomal segments larger than 20 Mb with unique or rare fl-LTR insertion patterns that were present in only 1 to 4 of the RQA genomes, of which 273 insertion patterns were uniquely associated with a single genome (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">13</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">16</a>). The majority of unique regions were in PI190962 (spelt wheat; <i>Triticum aestivum</i> ssp. <i>spelta</i>), which was expected, given that it diverged from modern bread wheat several thousand years ago.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Introgressions and large-scale structural variation in wheat."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Introgressions and large-scale structural variation in wheat.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="701" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>–<b>c</b>, <i>T. ponticum</i> introgression on chromosome 3D in LongReach Lancer (<b>a</b>), <i>T. timopheevi</i> introgression on chromosome 2B in LongReach Lancer (<b>b</b>) and <i>A. ventricosa</i> introgression on chromosome 3D in Jagger (<b>c</b>). Track i, map of polymorphic RLC-<i>Angela</i> retrotransposon insertions (legend at bottom); track ii, density of projected gene annotations from Chinese Spring (blue bars, scaled to maximum value); track iii, per cent identity to Chinese Spring based on chromosome alignment (yellow; scale is 0–100%); track iv, read depth of wheat wild relatives (blue–yellow heat map; legend at bottom). <b>d</b>, Dot plot alignment showing chromosome-level collinearity (black) with relative density of CENH3 ChIP–seq mapped to 100-kb bins for Chinese Spring (blue) and Julius (red); the arrow indicates a centromere shift. <b>e</b>, Robertsonian translocation between chromosomes 5B and 7B in Arina<i>LrFor</i>. <b>f</b>, <b>g</b>, Cytology (<b>f</b>) and Hi-C (<b>g</b>) confirm the 5B/7B translocation in SY Mattis (left) compared with the non-carrier Norin 61 (right). In <b>f</b>, five independent cells were observed; the translocation was confirmed independently ten times. Scale bar, 10 μm.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A similar strategy was used to confirm RLC-<i>Angela</i> variation at the telomeric region of chromosome 2A in Jagger, Mace, SY Mattis and CDC Stanley (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig2">2c</a>), which corresponds to the 2NvS introgression from <i>Aegilops ventricosa</i> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">5</a>). This introgression is a well-known source of resistance to wheat blast<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Cruz, C. D. et al. The 2NS translocation from Aegilops ventricosa confers resistance to the Triticum pathotype of Magnaporthe oryzae. Crop Sci. 56, 990–1000 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2756">30</a></sup>, and contains the <i>Lr37–Yr17–Sr38</i> gene cluster, which provides resistance to several rust diseases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Helguera, M. et al. PCR assays for the Lr37-Yr17-Sr38 cluster of rust resistance genes and their use to develop isogenic hard red spring wheat lines. Crop Sci. 43, 1839–1847 (2003)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2764">31</a></sup>. Sequencing of <i>A. ventricosa</i> accessions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">12</a>) followed by comparison of chromosomes with the RQAs confirmed that Jagger, Mace, SY Mattis and CDC Stanley carry the 2NvS introgression, which spans about 33 Mb on chromosome 2A (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig2">2c</a>, Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig9">6a</a>). We annotated the coding genes within this region and identified 535 high-confidence genes; more than 10% were predicted to be associated with disease resistance, including genes that encode putative NB-ARC and NLRs (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig9">6b</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">18</a>). Furthermore, we used genotyping by sequencing to detect the 2NvS segment in three wheat panels and discovered that its frequency has been increasing in breeding germplasm and its presence is consistently associated with higher grain yield (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig9">6c, d</a>, Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">19</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">20</a>). Of note, we identified about 60 genes belonging to the cytochrome P450 superfamily, which have been implicated in abiotic and biotic stress tolerance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Li, Y. &amp; Wei, K. Comparative functional genomics analysis of cytochrome P450 gene superfamily in wheat and maize. BMC Plant Biol. 20, 93 (2020)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2800">32</a></sup> and have been functionally validated to influence grain yield in wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Gunupuru, L. R. et al. A wheat cytochrome P450 enhances both resistance to deoxynivalenol and grain yield. PLoS ONE 13, e0204992 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2804">33</a></sup>. Together, these data indicate that the modern wheat gene pool contains many chromosomal segments of diverse ancestral origins, which can be identified by their transposable-element signatures. We also confirmed the wild-relative origins of three introgressions within the RQA assemblies—a first step towards characterizing causal genes for breeding targets, such as resistance to wheat blast and rust fungi.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Centromere dynamics"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Centromere dynamics</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>Centromeres are vital for cell division and chromosome pairing during meiosis. In plants, functional centromeres are defined by the epigenetic placement of the modified histone CENH3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, B. et al. Wheat centromeric retrotransposons: the new ones take a major role in centromeric structure. Plant J. 73, 952–965 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2817">34</a></sup>. We therefore used CENH3 chromatin immunoprecipitation and sequencing (ChIP–seq)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Gent, J. I., Wang, K., Jiang, J. &amp; Dawe, R. K. Stable patterns of CENH3 occupancy through maize lineages containing genetically similar centromeres. Genetics 200, 1105–1116 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2821">35</a></sup> to determine the positions and sizes (about 7.5–9.6 Mb) of the centromeres for each RQA (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">21</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">22</a>), which were consistent with previous estimates for wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2831">1</a></sup>. Furthermore, all chromosomes showed a single active site, implying that previous reports of multiple active centromeres in Chinese Spring<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2836">1</a></sup> were artefacts of misoriented scaffolds. However, we found examples in which the relative position of the centromere was shifted owing to several pericentric inversions, including inversions on chromosomes 4B and 5B (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig10">7a, b</a>). We also observed one instance in which the centromeric position changed, but was not associated with a structural event. Specifically, on chromosome 4D in Chinese Spring, the centromere is shifted by around 25 Mb relative to the consensus position (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig2">2d</a>). This shift was previously recognized by cytology but was hypothesized to result from a pericentric inversion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Koo, D. H., Sehgal, S. K., Friebe, B. &amp; Gill, B. S. Structure and stability of telocentric chromosomes in wheat. PLoS ONE 10, e0137747 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2846">36</a></sup>. However, the high degree of collinearity between genomes supports the hypothesis that Cen4D in Chinese Spring has shifted to a non-homologous position; this shifting of centromeres to non-homologous sites has also been reported in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Schneider, K. L., Xie, Z., Wolfgruber, T. K. &amp; Presting, G. G. Inbreeding drives maize centromere evolution. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E987–E996 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2850">37</a></sup>. By characterizing the centromere positions for these diverse wheat lines, we provide strong evidence for changes in centromere position caused by structural rearrangements and centromere shifts.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Large-scale structural variation between genomes"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Large-scale structural variation between genomes</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><p>Structural variants are common in wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Saxena, R. K., Edwards, D. &amp; Varshney, R. K. Structural variations in plant genomes. Brief. Funct. Genomics 13, 296–307 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2862">38</a></sup>, and impact genome structure and gene content. We characterized large structural variants using pairwise genome alignments (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig4">1</a>), changes in three-dimensional topology of chromosomes revealed by Hi-C conformation capture directionality biases along the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Harewood, L. et al. Hi-C as a tool for precise detection and characterisation of chromosomal rearrangements and copy number variation in human tumours. Genome Biol. 18, 125 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2869">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Himmelbach, A. et al. Discovery of multi-megabase polymorphic inversions by chromosome conformation capture sequencing in large-genome plant species. Plant J. 96, 1309–1316 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2872">40</a></sup> (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig11">8</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">23</a>), which were confirmed by Oxford Nanopore long-read sequencing (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig5">2</a>) and cytological karyotyping (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig10">7c</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">24</a>, Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">6</a>). The most prominent event was a translocation between chromosomes 5B and 7B, observed in Arina<i>LrFor</i>, SY Mattis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig2">2e–g</a>) and Claire. Normally, chromosomes 5B and 7B are approximately 737 and 762 Mb long, respectively, and we estimated that the recombined chromosomes are 488 Mb (5BS/7BS) and 993 Mb (7BL/5BL) long, making 7BL/5BL the largest wheat chromosome (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig12">9a</a>). In Arina<i>LrFor</i> and SY Mattis, the 7BL/5BL breakpoint resides within an approximately 5-kb GAA microsatellite, which we were able to span using polymerase chain reaction (PCR) (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig12">9b, c</a>). By contrast, the breakpoint on 5BS/7BS was less syntenic, and we detected polymorphic fluorescence in situ hybridization signals between Arina<i>LrFor</i> and SY Mattis on the 5BS portion of the translocated chromosome segment, suggesting that the regions adjacent to the translocation events differ on 5BS/7BS (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">6</a>). To determine the stability of the translocation in breeding, we genotyped for the translocation event in a panel of 538 wheat lines that represent most of the UK wheat gene pool grown since the 1920s<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Fradgley, N. et al. A large-scale pedigree resource of wheat reveals evidence for adaptation and selection by breeders. PLoS Biol. 17, e3000071 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2917">41</a></sup>. The translocation occurred in 66% of the lines and was selectively neutral (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">7</a>). Notably, the <i>Ph1</i> locus on chromosome 5B, which controls the pairing of homeologous chromosomes during meiosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Martín, A. C., Rey, M. D., Shaw, P. &amp; Moore, G. Dual effect of the wheat Ph1 locus on chromosome synapsis and crossover. Chromosoma 126, 669–680 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2928">42</a></sup>, is near the translocation breakpoint, but remained highly syntenic between translocation carriers and non-carriers. Genetic mapping and analysis of short-read sequencing data indicated that the 5B/7B translocated chromosomes recombine freely with 5B and 7B chromosomes (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig12">9d</a>), suggesting that chromosome pairing is not affected by the translocation.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Haplotype-based gene mapping"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Haplotype-based gene mapping</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>To develop improved wheat cultivars, breeders shuffle allelic variants by making targeted crosses and exploiting the recombination that occurs during meiosis. These alleles, however, are not inherited independently, but rather as haplotype blocks that often extend across multiple genes that are in genetic linkage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Bevan, M. W. et al. Genomic innovation for crop improvement. Nature 543, 346–354 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2943">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Luján Basile, S. M. et al. Haplotype block analysis of an Argentinean hexaploid wheat collection and GWAS for yield components and adaptation. BMC Plant Biol. 19, 553 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2946">44</a></sup>. We quantified haplotype variation along chromosomes across the assemblies, and developed visualization software to support its utility (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">8</a>). We used these haplotypes to characterize a locus that provides resistance to the orange wheat blossom midge (OWBM, <i>Sitodiplosis mosellana</i> Géhin), one of the most damaging insect pests of wheat, which is endemic in Europe, North America, west Asia and the Far East. Upon hatching, the first-instar larvae feed on the developing grains and damage the kernels (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig3">3a</a>). <i>Sm1</i> is the only gene in wheat known to provide resistance to OWBM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Kassa, M. T. et al. A saturated SNP linkage map for the orange wheat blossom midge resistance gene Sm1. Theor. Appl. Genet. 129, 1507–1517 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d23426e2963">6</a></sup>. CDC Landmark, Robigus and Paragon are all resistant to the OWBM, and all three carry the same 7.3-Mb haplotype within the <i>Sm1</i> locus on chromosome 2B (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig3">3b</a>). To identify <i>Sm1</i> gene candidates, we used high-resolution genetic mapping and refined the locus to a 587-kb interval in the CDC Landmark RQA (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig3">3c</a>, Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig13">10a</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">25</a>). Through extensive genotyping of diverse breeding lines, we found an OWBM-susceptible line, Waskada, that displayed a resistant haplotype except near one gene, which we annotated in CDC Landmark to encode a canonical NLR with kinase and major sperm protein (MSP) integrated domains (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig3">3c</a>). Oxford Nanopore long-read sequencing further confirmed the structure of the gene in CDC Landmark (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig13">10b</a>). By contrast, the remaining assemblies (susceptible to OWBM) lacked the NB-ARC domain, but the kinase and MSP domains remained intact (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig3">3c</a>). We sequenced the Waskada allele and found it contains the NB-ARC domain, but an alternative haplotype within the kinase domain (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig3">3c</a>, Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig13">10c</a>). This gene is expressed in wheat kernels and seedlings of <i>Sm1</i> carrier lines, and the lack of cDNA amplification of the NB-ARC domain for non-carrier lines further supported an alternative gene structure (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig13">10c</a>). We generated two knockout-mutant lines of this candidate gene in the <i>Sm1</i> carrier line Unity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Fox, S. L. et al. Unity hard red spring wheat. Can. J. Plant Sci. 90, 71–78 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3011">45</a></sup>, and both were consistently rated as susceptible to OWBM (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">26</a>). Sequencing of the candidate gene in these two mutants revealed a single point mutation in each line: a G&gt;A mutation resulting in a Gly&gt;Arg (G182R) amino acid substitution in the NB-ARC domain, and a G&gt;A mutation, resulting in a stop codon (W98*) before the NB-ARC domain (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig3">3c</a>). The kinase domain encoded by <i>Sm1</i> belongs to the serine/threonine class<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Hanks, S. K., Quinn, A. M. &amp; Hunter, T. The protein kinase family: conserved features and deduced phylogeny of the catalytic domains. Science 241, 42–52 (1988)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3025">46</a></sup>, similar to those of <i>Rpg5</i>, which provides stem rust resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Brueggeman, R. et al. The stem rust resistance gene Rpg5 encodes a protein with nucleotide-binding-site, leucine-rich, and protein kinase domains. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 105, 14970–14975 (2008)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3032">47</a></sup>, and <i>Tsn1</i>, which encodes sensitivity to the necrotrophic effector ToxA produced by <i>Parastagonospora nodorum</i> and <i>Pyrenophora tritici-repentis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Faris, J. D. et al. A unique wheat disease resistance-like gene governs effector-triggered susceptibility to necrotrophic pathogens. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 107, 13544–13549 (2010)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3045">48</a></sup>; however, both <i>Rpg5</i> and <i>Tsn1</i> lack the MSP domain. To our knowledge, this is the first report of an NB-ARC-LRR-kinase-MSP coding gene associated with insect resistance. Additional research is needed to functionally validate these domains and their putative role in OWBM resistance using tools such as gene editing. Nevertheless, we developed a high-throughput and low-cost competitive allele-specific PCR marker (KASP) that discriminates between OWBM-susceptible and OWBM-resistant lines with perfect accuracy (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#Fig13">10d</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">27</a>). Our analyses, along with the haplotype and synteny viewers (<a href="https://kiranbandi.github.io/10wheatgenomes/">https://kiranbandi.github.io/10wheatgenomes/</a>, <a href="http://10wheatgenomes.plantinformatics.io/">http://10wheatgenomes.plantinformatics.io/</a> and <a href="http://www.crop-haplotypes.com/">http://www.crop-haplotypes.com/</a>), laid the foundation for identifying haplotypes for <i>Sm1</i>. Haplotypes can now be genotyped in breeding programs using single-marker or high-throughput-sequencing-based approaches, which can integrate desirable genes into improved cultivars more efficiently.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Cloning of the gene Sm1."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Cloning of the gene <i>Sm1</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="307" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, The orange wheat blossom midge oviposits eggs on wheat spikes and the larvae feed on developing wheat grains, resulting in moderate to severe damage to mature kernels. <b>b</b>, Top, sections of chromosome 2B of the same colour in the same position share haplotypes (based on 5-Mb bins), with the exception of those in grey, which indicates a line-specific haplotype. The position of <i>Sm1</i> is indicated with respect to the CDC Landmark assembly. Bottom, zoomed-in view of haplotype blocks (based on 250-kb bins) from 5 to 25 Mb positions on chromosome 2B, surrounding <i>Sm1</i>. CDC Landmark, Robigus and Paragon all carry the same haplotype surrounding <i>Sm1</i> (teal). <b>c</b>, Top, anchoring of the <i>Sm1</i> fine map to the physical maps of Chinese Spring and CDC Landmark and graphical genotypes of three haplotypes critical to localizing the <i>Sm1</i> candidate gene. Bottom, annotation of the <i>Sm1</i> candidate gene, which encodes NB-ARC and LRR motifs in addition to the integrated serine/threonine (S/T) kinase and MSP domains. Two independent ethyl-methanesulfonate-induced mutations (W98* and G182R) result in loss of function and susceptibility to the orange wheat blossom midge (light blue lines). An alternative haplotype was observed in the kinase region of Waskada (black).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>We have built on the genome-sequence resources available for wheat and related species to produce ten RQAs and five scaffolded assemblies that represent hexaploid wheat lines from different regions, growth habits and breeding programs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="The International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3145">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93–97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3148">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Maccaferri, M. et al. Durum wheat genome highlights past domestication signatures and future improvement targets. Nat. Genet. 51, 885–895 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3151">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427–433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3154">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Jordan, K. W. et al. A haplotype map of allohexaploid wheat reveals distinct patterns of selection on homoeologous genomes. Genome Biol. 16, 48 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3157">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Luo, M. C. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of the wheat D genome Aegilops tauschii. Nature 551, 498–502 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3160">49</a></sup>. We have identified and characterized SNPs, PAV, CNV, centromere shifts, large-scale structural variants and introgressions from wild relatives of wheat that can be used to identify and characterize important breeding targets. This was complemented by a transposable-element-analysis approach to identify candidate introgressions from wild relatives of wheat, for which we provided high-quality assemblies of segments already used in global breeding programs. Together, these RQAs present an opportunity for breeders and researchers to perform high-resolution manipulation of genomic segments and pave the way to identifying genes responsible for in-demand traits, as we demonstrated for resistance to the insect pest OWBM. Functional gene studies will also be facilitated by comparative gene analyses, as exemplified by our analyses of orthologous groups, <i>Rf</i> genes and NLR immune receptors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Keller, B., Wicker, T. &amp; Krattinger, S. G. Advances in wheat and pathogen genomics: Implications for disease control. Annu. Rev. Phytopathol. 56, 67–87 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3167">26</a></sup>. Finally, we highlight haplotype blocks, which will facilitate marker development for applied breeding<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Bevan, M. W. et al. Genomic innovation for crop improvement. Nature 543, 346–354 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3171">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Borrill, P., Harrington, S. A. &amp; Uauy, C. Applying the latest advances in genomics and phenomics for trait discovery in polyploid wheat. Plant J. 97, 56–72 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3174">50</a></sup>. Equipped with multiple layers of data describing variation in wheat, we now have powerful tools to increase the rate of wheat improvement to meet future food demands.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>No statistical methods were used to predetermine sample size. The field experiments were randomized, but the wheat lines sequenced and assembled were not selected at random.
