The investigators were not blinded to allocation during experiments and outcome assessment.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">Assemblies and annotation</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec12">Genome assemblies</h4><p>We assembled the genomes of 15 diverse wheat lines using two approaches (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">1</a>). The RQA approach used the DeNovoMAGIC v.3.0 assembly pipeline, previously used for the wild emmer wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93–97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3200">11</a></sup>, durum wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Maccaferri, M. et al. Durum wheat genome highlights past domestication signatures and future improvement targets. Nat. Genet. 51, 885–895 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3204">12</a></sup> and Chinese Spring RefSeqv1.0 assemblies. In brief, high-molecular-weight DNA was extracted from wheat seedlings as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Dvorak, J., Mcguire, P. E. &amp; Cassidy, B. Apparent sources of the A genomes of wheats inferred from polymorphism in abundance and restriction fragment length of repeated nucleotide-sequences. Genome 30, 680–689 (1988)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3208">51</a></sup>. Illumina 450-bp paired-end (PE), 800-bp PE and mate-pair (MP) libraries of three different sizes (3 kb, 6 kb and 9 kb) were generated. Sequencing was performed at the University of Illinois Roy J. Carver Biotechnology Center. 10X Genomics Chromium libraries were prepared and sequenced at the Genome Canada Genome Innovation Centre using the manufacturers’ recommendations to achieve a minimum of 30 × coverage. Hi-C libraries were prepared using previously described methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Himmelbach, A. et al. Discovery of multi-megabase polymorphic inversions by chromosome conformation capture sequencing in large-genome plant species. Plant J. 96, 1309–1316 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3212">40</a></sup>. Using the Illumina PE, MP, 10X Genomics Chromium, and Hi-C, chromosome scale assemblies were prepared as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427–433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3217">18</a></sup>. For cultivars assembled to a scaffold level, we used the W2RAP-contigger using <i>k</i> = 200 (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">1</a>). Two MP libraries (10 kb and 13 kb) were produced for each line except Weebill 1, for which two additional MP libraries were used. Mate pairs were processed, filtered and used to scaffold contigs as described in the W2RAP pipeline (<a href="https://github.com/bioinfologics/w2rap">https://github.com/bioinfologics/w2rap</a>). Scaffolds of less than 500 bp were removed from the final assemblies. Additionally, we performed Oxford Nanopore sequencing of CDC Landmark using R9 flow cells and the GridION sequencing technology (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">2</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec13">Nucleotide diversity analysis</h4><p>The variant call format data files from two wheat exome-capture studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="He, F. et al. Exome sequencing highlights the role of wild-relative introgression in shaping the adaptive landscape of the wheat genome. Nat. Genet. 51, 896–904 (2019); correction 51, 1194 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3245">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Pont, C. et al. Tracing the ancestry of modern bread wheats. Nat. Genet. 51, 905–911 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3248">5</a></sup> were retrieved, combined, and filtered to retain hexaploid accessions and polymorphisms detected in both studies. The 10X Genomics Chromium sequencing data for each of the RQA lines were aligned to Chinese Spring RefSeqv1.0 using the LongRanger v.2.1.6 software. Alignment files from the accessions assembled here and 16 Bioplatforms Australia lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Edwards, D. et al. Bread matters: a national initiative to profile the genetic diversity of Australian wheat. Plant Biotechnol. J. 10, 703–708 (2012)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3252">19</a></sup> with alignments obtained from the DAWN project<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Watson-Haigh, N. S., Suchecki, R., Kalashyan, E., Garcia, M. &amp; Baumann, U. DAWN: a resource for yielding insights into the diversity among wheat genomes. BMC Genomics 19, 941 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3256">52</a></sup> were then used for variant calling by GATK v.3.8 at the same genomic positions identified by exome-capture sequencing. The variant files from the exome-capture studies, DAWN project and 10+ Wheat Genomes lines were then merged and subjected to principal component analysis (PCA) using the prcomp function in R v.3.6.1.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec14">Gene projections</h4><p>We used the previously published high-confidence gene models for Chinese Spring to assess the gene content in each assembly. Representative coding sequences of each informant locus were aligned to pseudomolecules of each line separately using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3268">53</a></sup> v.3.5 with the ‘fine’ parameter and a maximal intron size of 70 kb. BLAT matches seeded an additional alignment by exonerate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Slater, G. S. &amp; Birney, E. Automated generation of heuristics for biological sequence comparison. BMC Bioinformatics 6, 31 (2005)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3272">54</a></sup> in the genomic neighbourhood encompassing 20 kb upstream and downstream of the match position. Exonerate alignments required a minimal and maximal intron sizes of 30 bp and 20 kb, respectively. A linear regression of colocalized matches with complete alignments of the informant were computed for 10,000 such pairs to derive a normalization function and to render comparable scoring schemes for both methods. Subsequently, we selected the top-scoring match for each mapping pair as the locus for the gene projection. Projections were then filtered by alignment coverage (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">3</a>), the open reading frame (ORF) contiguity, the observed mapping frequency of the informant, coverage of start and stop codons, and the orthology or potential dislocation of the match scaffold relative to its informant chromosome. Identification of orthologous groups was analogous to the approach used previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Tatusov, R. L., Galperin, M. Y., Natale, D. A. &amp; Koonin, E. V. The COG database: a tool for genome-scale analysis of protein functions and evolution. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 33–36 (2000)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3279">55</a></sup>. Reciprocal best BLAST hit (RBH) graphs were derived from pairwise all-against-all BLASTn v2.8 transcript searches (minimal <i>e</i>-value ≤ 1 × 10<sup>−30</sup>). Hits were assigned to homeologous groups on the basis of gene models of Chinese Spring following a previously described homeologue classification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Dubcovsky, J. &amp; Dvorak, J. Genome plasticity a key factor in the success of polyploid wheat under domestication. Science 316, 1862–1866 (2007)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3289">9</a></sup>. Multiple sequence alignments for the population genetics analysis were performed using MUSCLE v.3.8 with default parameters (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">3</a>). Using the gene projections, we quantified average pairwise genetic diversity (<i>π</i>), polymorphism (Watterson’s <i>θ</i><sub>W</sub>), and Tajima’s <i>D</i> using compute and polydNdS in the libsequence v.1.0.3-1 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Thornton, K. Libsequence: a C++ class library for evolutionary genetic analysis. Bioinformatics 19, 2325–2327 (2003)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3307">56</a></sup>. We retained diversity estimates for genes that were in all of the genomes and had ≤100 segregating sites. PAV was determined from the orthologous groups limited to one-to-one relations where there was no match in at least one genome.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec15">Analysis of the <i>Rf</i>-like gene family</h4><p>For <i>Rf</i> genes, the genome sequences were scanned for ORFs in six frame translations with the getorf program of the EMBOSS v.6.6.0 package. ORFs longer than 89 codons were searched for the presence of PPR motifs using hmmsearch from the HMMER v.3.2.1 package (<a href="http://hmmer.org">http://hmmer.org</a>) and the hidden Markov models defined previously. The PF02536 profile from the Pfam v32.0 database (<a href="http://pfam.xfam.org">http://pfam.xfam.org</a>) was used to screen for ORFs carrying mTERF motifs. Downstream processing of the hmmsearch results followed the pipeline described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Cheng, S. et al. Redefining the structural motifs that determine RNA binding and RNA editing by pentatricopeptide repeat proteins in land plants. Plant J. 85, 532–547 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3340">57</a></sup>. ORFs with low hmmsearch scores were removed from the analysis as they are unlikely to represent functional PPR proteins. Only genes encoding mTERF proteins longer than 100 amino acids were included in the analysis. <i>RFL</i>-PPR sequences were identified as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Melonek, J., Stone, J. D. &amp; Small, I. Evolutionary plasticity of restorer-of-fertility-like proteins in rice. Sci. Rep. 6, 35152 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3348">23</a></sup>. The phylogenetic analyses were performed as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Melonek, J., Stone, J. D. &amp; Small, I. Evolutionary plasticity of restorer-of-fertility-like proteins in rice. Sci. Rep. 6, 35152 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3352">23</a></sup>. Conserved, non-PPR genes delimiting the borders of analysed <i>RFL</i> clusters were identified in the Chinese Spring RefSeqv1.0 reference genome and used to search for syntenic regions in the remaining wheat accessions with BLAST v.2.8. See Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">4</a> for more details.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec16">NLR repertoire</h4><p>NLR signatures were annotated using NLR-Annotator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Steuernagel, B. et al. Physical and transcriptional organisation of the bread wheat intracellular immune receptor repertoire. Preprint at &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1101/339424&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3371">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Steuernagel, B. et al. The NLR-Annotator tool enables annotation of the intracellular immune receptor repertoire. Plant Physiol. 183, 468–482 (2020)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3374">59</a></sup> (<a href="https://github.com/steuernb/NLR-Annotator">https://github.com/steuernb/NLR-Annotator</a>) with the option -a. We estimated redundancy of NLR signatures between genomes at different thresholds of identity: 95%, 98% and 100%. For the 165 amino acids in the consensus of all NB-ARC motifs, this translates to 8, 3 and 0 mismatches of a concatenated motif sequence. To calculate the overall redundancy in all genomes, we counted the number of LR signatures added to a non-redundant set by adding genomes iteratively. This was done for 1 million random permutations.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec17">Repeat annotation</h4><p>Transposons were detected and classified by a homology search against the REdat_9.7_Poaceae section of the PGSB transposon library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Spannagl, M. et al. PGSB PlantsDB: updates to the database framework for comparative plant genome research. Nucleic Acids Res. 44 (D1), D1141–D1147 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3393">60</a></sup> using vmatch (<a href="http://www.vmatch.de">http://www.vmatch.de</a>) with the following parameters: identity ≥70%, minimal hit length 75 bp, seedlength 12 bp (exact command line: -d -p -l 75 -identity 70 -seedlength 12 -exdrop 5). To remove overlapping annotations, the output was filtered for redundant hits via a priority-based approach in which higher-scoring matches where assigned first and lower-scoring hits at overlapping positions were either shortened or removed if there was ≥90% overlap with a priority hit or if &lt;50 bp remained. Tandem repeats where identified with TandemRepeatFinder v.4.09 under default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3404">61</a></sup> and subjected to overlap removal as described above. Full-length LTR retrotransposons were identified with LTRharvest (<a href="http://genometools.org/documents/ltrharvest.pdf">http://genometools.org/documents/ltrharvest.pdf</a>). All candidates were subsequently annotated for PfamA domains using HMMER v.3.0 and filtered to remove false positives, non-canonical hybrids and gene-containing elements. The inner domain order served as a criterion for the LTR retrotransposon superfamily classification, either Gypsy (RLG: RT-RH-INT), Copia (RLC: INT-RT-RH) or undetermined (RLX). The insertion age of fl-LTRs was calculated from the divergence between the 5′ and 3′ long terminal repeats, which are identical upon insertion. The genetic distance was calculated with EMBOSS v.6.6.0 distmat (Kimura2-parameter correction) using a random mutation rate of 1.3 × 10<sup>−8</sup>.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec18">Analysis of centromeric regions</h4><p>For each line with a RQA, ChIP was performed according to previous methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Nagaki, K. et al. Chromatin immunoprecipitation reveals that the 180-bp satellite repeat is the key functional DNA element of Arabidopsis thaliana centromeres. Genetics 163, 1221–1225 (2003)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3425">62</a></sup> with slight modification using a wheat-specific CENH3 antibody<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Koo, D. H., Sehgal, S. K., Friebe, B. &amp; Gill, B. S. Structure and stability of telocentric chromosomes in wheat. PLoS ONE 10, e0137747 (2015)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3429">36</a></sup>. An antigen with the peptide sequence RTKHPAVRKTKALPKK, corresponding to the N terminus of wheat CENH3, was used to produce an antibody using the custom-antibody production facility provided by Thermo Fisher Scientific. The customized antibody was purified and obtained as pellets. The antibody pellet (0.396 mg) was dissolved in 2 ml PBS buffer, pH 7.4, resulting in a working concentration of 198 ng μl<sup>−1</sup>. Nuclei were isolated from 2-week-old seedlings, digested with micrococcal nuclease and incubated overnight at 4 °C with 3 μg of antibody or rabbit serum (control). Antibodies were captured using Dynabeads Protein G and the chromatin eluted using 100 μl of 1% sodium dodecyl sulfate, 0.1 M NaHCO<sub>3</sub> preheated to 65 °C. DNA isolation was then performed using ChIP DNA Clean &amp; Concentrator Kit, and ChIP–seq libraries were constructed using TruSeq ChIP Library Preparation Kit and sequenced with a NovoSeq S4, which generated 150-bp paired-end reads.</p><p>For Chinese Spring, we used two datasets, SRR1686799<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Guo, X. et al. De novo centromere formation and centromeric sequence expansion in wheat and its wide hybrids. PLoS Genet. 12, e1005997 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3440">63</a></sup> (dataset 1) and the dataset generated in this study (dataset 2). Sequence reads were de-multiplexed, trimmed and aligned to each of the respective RQAs using HISAT2 v.2.1.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Kim, D., Paggi, J. M., Park, C., Bennett, C. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Graph-based genome alignment and genotyping with HISAT2 and HISAT-genotype. Nat. Biotechnol. 37, 907–915 (2019)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3444">64</a></sup>. Alignments were sorted, filtered for minimum alignment quality of 30, counted in 100-kb bins using samtools v.1.10 and BEDtools v.2.29, and visualized in R v.3.6.1. To define the midpoint of each centromere, we identified the highest density of CENH3 ChIP–seq reads using a smoothing spline in R v.3.6.1 with smooth.spline function (number of knots = 1,000) and identified the peak of the smooth spline as the centre of the respective centromere for a given chromosome. To compare centromeric positions of different genomes, the CENH3 ChIP–seq density was plotted along with MUMmer v.4.0 chromosome alignments. To determine the overall size of wheat centromeres, we considered each 100-kb bin with CENH3 ChIP–seq read density that was greater than three times the background (genome average) level of read density to be an active centromeric bin. The number of enriched bins for each genome were counted and averaged to a total of 21 chromosomes. This calculation included counting of unanchored bins.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Analysis of introgressions</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec20">Identification of full-length RLC-<i>Angela</i> retrotransposons</h4><p>Retrotransposon profiles were created for each genome using the RLC-<i>Angela</i> family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Wicker, T. et al. Impact of transposable elements on genome structure and evolution in bread wheat. Genome Biol. 19, 103 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3468">65</a></sup> and consensus sequences obtained from the TREP database (<a href="http://www.botinst.uzh.ch/en/research/genetics/thomasWicker/trep-db.html">www.botinst.uzh.ch/en/research/genetics/thomasWicker/trep-db.html</a>). First, BLASTn was used to compare the ~1,700-bp LTR of RLC-<i>Angela</i> to each genome. Matching elements and 500 bp of flanking sequences were aligned to identify precise LTR borders as well as different sub-families and/or sequences variants. We then used BLASTn to compare the 18 consensus LTR sequences against each genome and then screened for pairs of full-length LTRs that are found in the same orientation within a window of 7.5–9.5 kb (RLC-<i>Angela</i> elements are ~8.7 kb long). These initial candidate full-length elements were screened for the presence of RLC-<i>Angela</i> polyprotein sequences by BLASTx, as well as for the typical 5-bp target-site duplications. We allowed a maximum of two mismatches between the two target-site duplications. All identified full-length RLC-<i>Angela</i> copies were then aligned to a RLC-<i>Angela</i> consensus sequence with the program Water from the EMBOSS v.6.6.0 package (<a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/Tools/emboss/">www.ebi.ac.uk/Tools/emboss/</a>). These alignments were used to compile all nucleotide polymorphisms into a single file. The variant call file was then used for PCA using the snpgdsPCA function in the R package SNPrelate v.3.11.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec21">Sequencing of the tertiary gene pool of wheat</h4><p>Genomic DNA (gDNA) was extracted and purified from young leaf tissue collected from multiple accessions of <i>T. timopheevii</i>, <i>A. ventricosa</i> and <i>T. ponticum</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">12</a>) following a standard CTAB–chloroform extraction method. Yield and integrity were evaluated by fluorometry (Qubit 2.0) and agarose gel electrophoresis. Paired-end libraries were prepared following the Nextera DNA Flex protocol. In brief, 500 ng gDNA from each accession was fragmented and amplified with a limited-cycle PCR. Each library was uniquely dual-indexed with a distinct 10-bp index code (IDT for Illumina Nextera DNA UD) for multiplexing, and quantified by qPCR (Kapa Biosystems). Final average library size was estimated on a Tapestation 2200. Libraries were normalized and pooled for sequencing on an Illumina NovaSeq 6000 S4 to generate ~5× coverage per genotype. Sequencing data were de-multiplexed and aligned to appropriate RQAs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">12</a>) in semi-perfect mode using the BBMap v.38 short-read alignment software (<a href="https://sourceforge.net/projects/bbmap/">https://sourceforge.net/projects/bbmap/</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Structural variation</h3><p>We karyotyped the lines using mitotic metaphase chromosomes prepared by the conventional acetocarmine-squash method. Non-denaturing fluorescence in situ hybridization (ND-FISH) of three repetitive sequence probes, Oligo-pSc119.2-1, Oligo-pTa535 and Oligo-pTa713, was performed as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Tang, Z., Yang, Z. &amp; Fu, S. Oligonucleotides replacing the roles of repetitive sequences pAs1, pSc119.2, pTa-535, pTa71, CCS1, and pAWRC.1 for FISH analysis. J. Appl. Genet. 55, 313–318 (2014)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3542">66</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Zhao, L. et al. Cytological identification of an Aegilops variabilis chromosome carrying stripe rust resistance in wheat. Breed. Sci. 66, 522–529 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3545">67</a></sup> (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">6</a>). Chromosomes were counterstained with DAPI. Chromosome images were captured with an Olympus BX61 epifluorescence microscope and a CCD camera DP80. Images were processed and pseudocoloured with ImageJ v.1.51n in the Fiji package. For karyotyping, at least four chromosomes per accession were examined and compared to the karyotype of Chinese Spring as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Komuro, S., Endo, R., Shikata, K. &amp; Kato, A. Genomic and chromosomal distribution patterns of various repeated DNA sequences in wheat revealed by a fluorescence in situ hybridization procedure. Genome 56, 131–137 (2013)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3552">68</a></sup>. Hierarchal clustering of karyotype polymorphisms was performed using the Ward method in R v.3.0.2, which was used to estimate distance. Next, we applied Hi-C analysis for inversion calling as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Himmelbach, A. et al. Discovery of multi-megabase polymorphic inversions by chromosome conformation capture sequencing in large-genome plant species. Plant J. 96, 1309–1316 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3556">40</a></sup>. In brief, adapters were removed and reads were mapped to Chinese Spring using minimap2 v.2.10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Li, H. Minimap2: pairwise alignment for nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 34, 3094–3100 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3560">69</a></sup> as we have done previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Paape, T. et al. Patterns of polymorphism and selection in the subgenomes of the allopolyploid Arabidopsis kamchatica. Nat. Commun. 9, 3909 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3565">21</a></sup>. The raw Hi-C link counts were calculated in 1 Mb non-overlapping sliding windows and then normalized as described in our previous work<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Himmelbach, A. et al. Discovery of multi-megabase polymorphic inversions by chromosome conformation capture sequencing in large-genome plant species. Plant J. 96, 1309–1316 (2018)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3569">40</a></sup>. Finally, the normalized Hi-C link matrix was subjected to inversion calling using R.</p><p>We performed flow cytometry of wheat cultivars Arina and Forno as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Kubaláková, M., Vrána, J., Cíhalíková, J., Simková, H. &amp; Doležel, J. Flow karyotyping and chromosome sorting in bread wheat (Triticum aestivum L.). Theor. Appl. Genet. 104, 1362–1372 (2002)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3576">70</a></sup>, except that we used a FACSAria SORP flow cytometer and cell sorter (Becton Dickinson). The 5B/7B translocation breakpoints were identified by comparison of chromosomes 5B and 7B from Arina<i>LrFor</i> and Julius. Sequence collinearity between Arina<i>LrFor</i> and Julius was detected by BLASTn searches of 1,000-bp sequence windows every 100 kb along the chromosomes. Once an interruption of synteny was detected, sequence segments at the positions of synteny loss were extracted and used for local alignments to determine the precise breakpoint positions. PCR amplification of the 5BS/7BS and 7BL/5BL translocation sites was performed using standard PCR cycling conditions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec23">Characterization of haplotypes</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec24">Development of a wheat genome haplotype database</h4><p>To identify haplotypes, pairwise chromosome alignments were performed between the RQA using MUMmer v.4.0, which were combined with pairwise nucleotide BLASTn analyses of the genes ± 2,000 bp using custom scripts in R v.3.6.1 (<a href="https://github.com/Uauy-Lab/pangenome-haplotypes">https://github.com/Uauy-Lab/pangenome-haplotypes</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Brinton, J. et al. A haplotype-led approach to increase the precision of wheat breeding. Commun. Biol. https://doi.org/10.1038/s42003-020-01413-2 (2020)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3606">71</a></sup> (Supplementary Note 8). The resultant haplotypes were uploaded to an interactive viewer (<a href="http://www.crop-haplotypes.com/">http://www.crop-haplotypes.com/</a>). Pairwise BLASTn comparisons of the genes were also used to identify structural variants, and were uploaded into AccuSyn (<a href="https://accusyn.usask.ca/">https://accusyn.usask.ca/</a>) and SynVisio (<a href="https://synvisio.github.io/#/">https://synvisio.github.io/#/</a>) to create a wheat-specific database (<a href="https://kiranbandi.github.io/10wheatgenomes/">https://kiranbandi.github.io/10wheatgenomes/</a>). Pretzel (<a href="https://github.com/plantinformatics/pretzel">https://github.com/plantinformatics/pretzel</a>) was also used to visualize and compare the RQA and the projected gene annotations (<a href="http://10wheatgenomes.plantinformatics.io/">http://10wheatgenomes.plantinformatics.io/</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec25">Characterization of <i>Sm1</i>
                           </h4><p><i>Sm1</i>-linked markers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Kassa, M. T. et al. A saturated SNP linkage map for the orange wheat blossom midge resistance gene Sm1. Theor. Appl. Genet. 129, 1507–1517 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3667">6</a></sup> were located in RQAs using BLAST v.2.8.0. Two high-resolution mapping populations were developed, 99B60-EJ2D/Thatcher and 99B60-EJ2G/Infinity. Progeny heterozygous for crossover events near <i>Sm1</i> were identified in the F<sub>2</sub> generation, and the crossovers were fixed in the F<sub>3</sub> generation. The resulting F<sub>2</sub>-derived F<sub>3</sub> families were analysed with KASP markers within the <i>Sm1</i> region and tested for resistance to OWBM in field nurseries to identify markers associated with <i>Sm1</i>. Ethyl methanesulfonate was used to develop knockout mutants in the <i>Sm1</i> gene. Approximately 3,200 seeds of the Canadian spring wheat variety Unity (an <i>Sm1</i> carrier) were soaked in a 0.2% (v/v) aqueous ethyl methanesulfonate solution for 22 h at 22 °C. The seed was then rinsed in distilled water and sown in a field nursery. The M<sub>1</sub> seed was grown to maturity and bulk harvested. Approximately 6,000 M<sub>2</sub> seeds were space planted in two field nurseries located in Brandon and Glenlea, Manitoba, Canada. Spikes were collected on a per-plant basis at maturity and were classified as resistant, susceptible or undamaged as done previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Kassa, M. T. et al. A saturated SNP linkage map for the orange wheat blossom midge resistance gene Sm1. Theor. Appl. Genet. 129, 1507–1517 (2016)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3700">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Thomas, J. et al. Chromosome location and markers of Sm1: a gene of wheat that conditions antibiotic resistance to orange wheat blossom midge. Mol. Breed. 15, 183–192 (2005)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3703">72</a></sup>. Putative <i>Sm1</i>-knockout mutants were re-tested for OWBM resistance in indoor cage tests<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Lamb, R. J. et al. Resistance to Sitodiplosis mosellana (Diptera: Cecidomyiidae) in spring wheat (Gramineae). Can. Entomol. 132, 591–605 (2000)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3710">73</a></sup> in the M<sub>3</sub> and M<sub>4</sub> generations. M<sub>4</sub>-derived families were tested for resistance to OWBM in field nurseries (randomized complete block design, six environments, and eight replicates per environment).</p><p>Candidate genes were identified between <i>Sm1</i> flanking markers on the CDC Landmark assembly using the projected gene annotations and FGENESH v.2.6 (<a href="http://www.softberry.com/">http://www.softberry.com/</a>), which were compared to the projected genes of non-carriers. Both 5′ and 3′ rapid amplification of cDNA ends (5′ and 3′ RACE) were used to verify the transcription initiation and termination sites of the gene candidate, whose structure was predicted by FGENESH v.2.6. In brief, RNA was extracted from the leaves of Unity (<i>Sm1</i> carrier) seedlings (using the Qiagen RNeasy kit), RACE PCR performed (Invitrogen GeneRacer kit), and the PCR product cloned (Invitrogen TOPO TA Cloning kit for sequencing) and sequenced by Sanger sequencing. Prediction of the conserved domains was done using the NCBI Conserved Domain Search tool (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/Structure/cdd/wrpsb.cgi">https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/Structure/cdd/wrpsb.cgi</a>) and PROSITE (release 2020_01; <a href="https://prosite.expasy.org/">https://prosite.expasy.org/</a>). The LRR domain was defined on the basis of the presence of 2–42 LRR motif repeats of 20–30 amino acids each. LRR motifs were manually annotated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="la Cour, T. et al. Analysis and prediction of leucine-rich nuclear export signals. Protein Eng. Des. Sel. 17, 527–536 (2004)." href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d23426e3752">74</a></sup>. Prediction of transmembrane regions and orientation was performed using the program TMpred NCBI Conserved Domain Search tool (<a href="https://embnet.vital-it.ch/software/TMPRED_form.html">https://embnet.vital-it.ch/software/TMPRED_form.html</a>).</p><p>To study the expression of <i>Sm1</i>, total RNA was extracted from four biological replicates from four wheat genotypes (Unity, CDC Landmark, Waskada and Thatcher) from two different tissues; seedling leaves and developing kernels (five days post anthesis) using NucleoSpin RNA Plant kit (Macherey-Nagel) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. RNA was treated with RNase-free DNase (rDNase) (Macherey-Nagel) and reversed transcribed into cDNA using SuperScript IV Reverse Transcriptase kit (Invitrogen) according to the manufacturer’s instructions and the NB-ARC domain amplified by PCR.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec26">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this paper.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>All sequence reads assemblies have been deposited into the National Center for Biotechnology Information sequence read archive (SRA) (see Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM4">1</a> for accession numbers). Sequence reads for the RQAs, <i>T. ponticum</i>, <i>A. ventricosa</i> and <i>T. timopheevii</i> have been deposited into the SRA (accession no. <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=PRJNA544491">PRJNA544491</a>) and ChIP–seq short read-data used for centromere characterization is deposited under accession no. <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=PRJNA625537">PRJNA625537</a>. All Hi-C data have been deposited in the European Nucleotide Archive (Supplementary Table 1). The RQAs are available for direct user download at <a href="https://wheat.ipk-gatersleben.de/">https://wheat.ipk-gatersleben.de/</a>. All assemblies and projected annotations are available for comparative analysis at Ensembl Plants (<a href="https://plants.ensembl.org/index.html">https://plants.ensembl.org/index.html</a>). Comparative analysis viewers are also online for synteny (<a href="https://kiranbandi.github.io/10wheatgenomes/">https://kiranbandi.github.io/10wheatgenomes/</a>, <a href="http://10wheatgenomes.plantinformatics.io/">http://10wheatgenomes.plantinformatics.io/</a>) and haplotypes (<a href="http://www.crop-haplotypes.com/">http://www.crop-haplotypes.com/</a>). Seed stocks of the assembled lines are available at the UK Germplasm Resources Unit (<a href="https://www.seedstor.ac.uk/">https://www.seedstor.ac.uk/</a>).</p>
            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>Code for custom genome visualizers have been deposited in the public domain for haplotype viewer (<a href="https://github.com/Uauy-Lab/pangenome-haplotypes">https://github.com/Uauy-Lab/pangenome-haplotypes</a>), Pretzel (<a href="https://github.com/plantinformatics/pretzel">https://github.com/plantinformatics/pretzel</a>), AccuSyn (<a href="https://github.com/jorgenunezsiri/accusyn">https://github.com/jorgenunezsiri/accusyn</a>) and SynVisio (<a href="https://github.com/kiranbandi/synvisio">https://github.com/kiranbandi/synvisio</a>). Additional scripts used for ChIP–seq analysis of the centromeres are provided at <a href="https://github.com/wheatgenetics/centromere">https://github.com/wheatgenetics/centromere</a>.</p>

                </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="J. Tameshige, T.B., D.C., M.H., R.S.-I., C.A., F.K., J.G.-G. and N.S.; SY Mattis: E.L. and A.B.; spelt (PI190962): A.D., C.J.P. and J.D.; Robigus, Claire, Paragon and Cadenza: M.B., M.C., B.C., C.F., N.F. and D.H.; Weebill 1: M.C., B.C., J.C., K.A.G., L.P.-A. and L.V. Sequencing, assembly and analysis were contributed by WRA2P computational assembly: A. Hall, B.C., G.G.A., K. Krasileva, N.M., D.S. and J. Wright; 10X Genomics: H.B., C.J.P., J.E., S.K. and K.W.; Hi-C and structural analysis: M.M., N.S., A. Himmelbach, C.M., S.P. and L.G.; pseudomolecule assemblies: M.M., C.M. and N.S.; gene projections and TE analysis: K.F.X.M., M.S., H.G. and G.H.; diversity and polymorphism analysis: K.K.S., E.D., T.P., G.H.-N., D.C., M.H., G.H., H.H., H.K., M.S., K.M., T. Tameshige, T. Tanaka, J.S. and J. Wu; centromere diversity: J.P. and D.H.K.; 5B/7B translocation: S.G.K., T.W., J.C. and M.C.K; 2N<sup>v</sup>S introgression: J.P., A.K.F., L.G., P.J., C.J.P., R.S. and S.W.; TE-based introgressions: T.W., B.B., J.E., M.C.K., J.P., C.J.P., J.T. and S.W; cytological karyotyping: S.N., K.M., Y.N., J.S. and T.K.; diversification of <i>Rf</i> genes: J.M. and I.S.; NLR repertoire: S.G.K. and B.S.; <i>Sm1</i> gene cloning: C.A.M., C.J.P., C.U., J.B., A.C.C., S.C., P.F., M.T.K., V.K., D.T. and K.W.; haplotype database: C.U., J.B. and R.H.R.-G.; visualization software: C.G., V.B., G.K.-G., J.N.S., J.T. and J.M.; BLAST server: M.M., A.F. and U.S.; C.J.P and S.W. drafted the manuscript with input from all authors. All co-authors contributed to and edited the final version.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to

            </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="additional-information" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="additional-information-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="additional-information">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="additional-information-content"><p><b>Peer review information</b> <i>Nature</i> thanks Victor Albert, Rudi Appels and the other, anonymous, reviewer(s) for their contribution to the peer review of this work.</p><p><b>Publisher’s note</b> Springer Nature remains neutral with regard to jurisdictional claims in published maps and institutional affiliations.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec28" data-title="Extended data figures and tables"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec28-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec28">Extended data figures and tables</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec28-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div id="figshareContainer" class="c-article-figshare-container" data-test="figshare-container"></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig4"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/4" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig4_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 1 Chromosome-scale collinearity between the RQA.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Genomes were aligned chromosome by chromosome using MUMmer and are represented as dot plots. The introgression on chromosome 2B of LongReach Lancer (red rectangles) and 5B/7B translocation in SY Mattis and Arina<i>LrFor</i> (purple rectangles) are indicated.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig5"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/5" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig5_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 2 Evaluation of the CDC Landmark RQA using Oxford Nanopore Long Reads.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Scaffold-scaffold long read contact map showing shared read IDs between scaffold ends along the ordered scaffolds in the CDC Landmark pseudomolecules. The diagonal pattern indicates that adjacent scaffolds share the same long reads and are therefore properly ordered and oriented by Hi-C in the RQA. <b>b</b>, Characterization of inversion events on chromosomes 2A, 3A, and 3D. The directionality biases estimated from alignments of Hi-C data against Chinese Spring (left, top), and chromosome alignment of the inversion events between CDC Landmark and Chinese Spring RQAs (left, bottom) are shown. Long reads spanning the inversion events and magnified views of the reads aligning to the left and right boundaries of the inversions (right) are provided.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig6"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/6" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig6_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 3 Diversity of genes and TEs.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Average pairwise genetic diversity of the homeologues (coding sequences only) of the A, B and D subgenomes. The mode of the A, B and D subgenome is 0.00057, 0.00082, and 0.0002, respectively. <b>b</b>, Tajima’s D estimates of coding sequences for each wheat subgenome. The lower and upper range of the boxplot hinges correspond to the first and third quartiles (the 25th and 75th percentiles). Boxplots show centre line, median; box limits, upper and lower quartiles; whiskers, 1.5 × interquartile range. <b>c</b>, Total gene counts and orthologues for the RQA. Genes in orthologous groups with exactly one gene for each line (Complete; dark brown), genes contained in unambiguous orthologous groups missing an orthologue for at least one line, that is, PAV (2-10 Lines; light brown), and genes with ambiguous orthologues or CNV (Other; pink) are indicated. <b>d</b>, Per cent of pairwise shared syntenic fl-LTRs between wheat lines.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig7"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/7" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig7_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 4 Evolutionary relationships among PPR and mTERF gene sequences.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, The <i>RFL</i> clade is in blue and all remaining P-class PPRs are in green. <b>b</b>, Clustered mTERF sequences are in blue and the remaining mTERFs are shown in green. The scale bar represents number of substitutions per site. <b>c</b>, Sequence inversions and copy number variation at the <i>Rf3</i> locus on chromosome 1B. <i>RFL</i> genes are shown as light pink triangles above the chromosome scale. Conserved non-PPR genes used as syntenic anchors are shown on the chromosome scale as coloured triangles. The total number (T) and the number of putatively functional <i>RFL</i> genes with 10 or more PPR motifs (F) are indicated on the right side of each panel.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 5 Identification of alien introgressions from wheat relatives.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>A feature of foreign chromosomal introgressions is that they contain unique patterns of TE insertions. Shown are stretches of &gt;20 Mb containing multiple polymorphic RLC-<i>Angela</i> retrotransposons that are found only in one or a few (≤4) of the sequenced lines. One representative chromosome for each wheat subgenome is shown. Individual polymorphic retrotransposons are indicated as coloured vertical lines. Colours correspond to the number of cultivars a foreign segment is found in. Regions of particular interest are indicated by black rectangles. These include the 2N<sup>v</sup>S alien introgression from <i>A. ventricosa</i> at the end of chromosome 2A in Jagger, Mace, SY Mattis and CDC Stanley, as well as introgression in the central region of chromosome 2B from <i>T. timopheevi</i> in LongReach Lancer, and introgression at the end of chromosome 3D from <i>T. ponticum</i> in LongReach Lancer.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig9"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/9" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig9_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 6 Detailed characterization of the 2N<sup>v</sup>S introgression from <i>A. ventricosa</i>.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Pairwise alignments of the first 50 Mb of chromosome 2A. The black arrow indicates a possible unique haplotype within spelt. <b>b</b>, Orthologous genes between the 2N<sup>v</sup>S introgression from <i>A. ventricosa</i> in Jagger and the genes on chromosomes 2A, 2B, and 2D in Chinese Spring. <b>c</b>, Frequency of 2N<sup>v</sup>S introgression carriers in North American datasets from CIMMYT, Kansas State, and the USDA Winter Wheat Regional Performance Nursery (RPN) over time. <b>d</b>, Per cent yield difference in lines that carry the 2N<sup>v</sup>S introgression. Two sided <i>t</i>-tests were performed to test for the significance of the impact of the 2N<sup>v</sup>S introgression. **<i>P</i> &lt; 0.01; ***<i>P</i> &lt; 0.001.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 7 Centromere positions and karyotype variation.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Functional centromere positions in the RQA have undergone structural and positional rearrangement. Chromosome alignments showing collinearity (black scaffolds in same orientation, grey scaffolds in opposite orientation) with relative density of CENH3 ChIP–seq mapped to 100 kb genomic bins for Chinese Spring (blue) and a representative genome of comparison (red) for chromosome 4B of CDC Stanley (<b>a</b>), and chromosome 5B of Julius (<b>b</b>). <b>c</b>, Detailed list and clustering of cytological features carried by each wheat line (Supplementary Note <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM3">6</a>). Features that are identical (dark grey) or have a gain (black) or loss (light grey) relative to Chinese Spring are indicated.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig11"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/11" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig11_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 8 Hi-C validates inversions identified from pairwise chromosome alignments.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Pairwise alignments of chromosome 6B from the RQA and Chinese Spring are shown. Above each alignment dot plot, the directionality biases estimated from alignments of Hi-C data against Chinese Spring are shown. Boundaries of diagonal segments are indicative of inversions and coincide with inversion boundaries identified from the chromosome alignments.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig12"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/12" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig12_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 9 Characterization of a translocation involving wheat chromosomes 5B and 7B.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Cytogenetic karyotypes of Forno (left) and Arina (right), the parents of Arina<i>LrFor</i>. Note that the large recombinant chromosome 7B is represented by a distinct peak. <b>b</b>, Sequence of the translocation breakpoint on chromosome 7B of Arina<i>LrFor</i>. Note that the exact breakpoint lies in a sequence gap (stretch of Ns). The bp positions are indicated at the left. Forward PCR primers are shown in red and reverse primers in blue. The overlap of the two reverse primers is shown in purple. The outer primer pair was used for PCR, while the inner pair was used for a nested PCR. <b>c</b>, PCR amplification of the fragment spanning the translocation breakpoint. The nested PCR yielded a ~5 kb fragment that spanned the translocation breakpoint and its identity was confirmed by sequencing. Both PCR and nested PCR were performed in duplicate; both replicates of the nested PCR were sequenced using the Sanger method. For gel source data, see Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x#MOESM1">1</a>. <b>d</b>, Mapping of Illumina reads from the cultivars Arina and Forno on to the pseudomolecules of Arina<i>LrFor</i>. Sequence derived from Forno is shown in blue, while sequenced derived from Arina is in red. Note that chromosomes 5B and 7B are derived from both parents, indicating that these parental chromosomes can recombine freely, despite the presence of a large 5B/7B translocation in Arina.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig13"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41586-020-2961-x/figures/13" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41586-020-2961-x/MediaObjects/41586_2020_2961_Fig13_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 10 Confirmation of gene expression and gene structure for <i>Sm1</i>.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Critical recombinants from the 99B60-EJ2G/Infinity and 99B60-EJ2D/Thatcher populations used to fine map <i>Sm1</i>. The 99B60-EJ2G/Infinity cross had 5,170 F<sub>2</sub> plants, while 99B60-EJ2D/Thatcher cross had 5,264 F<sub>2</sub> plants; only recombinant haplotypes between orange wheat blossom midge resistant (R) and susceptible (S) genotypes are shown. <b>b</b>, Oxford Nanopore long read confirmation of the <i>Sm1</i> gene candidate in the CDC Landmark RQA (left), and alternative haplotype in Chinese Spring (right). Vertical coloured lines indicate sequence variants. <b>c</b>, Amplification of cDNA for the NB-ARC domain of the <i>Sm1</i> gene candidate (top) and actin control (bottom) derived from RNA isolated from developing kernels (left) and wheat seedlings (right). Unity and CDC Landmark are carriers of <i>Sm1</i>. Waskada carries an alternative haplotype and does not carry <i>Sm1</i> (see main text). Thatcher was used as a susceptible parent for fine mapping of <i>Sm1</i> and does not contain the associated NB-ARC domain. The experiment was replicated on four independent biological samples for each condition. <b>d</b>, Distribution of an <i>Sm1</i> allele-specific PCR marker in a diverse panel of &gt;300 wheat lines.</p></div></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec29" data-title="Supplementary information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec29-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec29">Supplementary information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec29-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info">
